% which is precisely what we need for the induction step.
%  ?
% \bullet 
% Corollary 5.12. Let P be a bounded-above cochain complex of projectives of an
% abelian category A, and let L\bullet  be an exact complex in C(A).
% Then every morphism of complexes P \bullet  \to  L\bullet  is homotopic to 0.
% This follows immediately from (a harmless shift of) Lemma 5.11, since every
% morphism to an exact complex has no choice but to induce the zero-morphism in
% cohomology.
% The `injective' version of Corollary 5.12 is that morphisms from an exact com-
% plex L\bullet  to a bounded-below complex Q\bullet  of injectives are
% necessarily homotopy
% equivalent to 0.
% Note that in all these considerations the complexes P \bullet  , Q\bullet  are
% required to
% live in the `bounded' versions C- (P), C +(I): all objects should vanish in
% degree
% sufficiently high or low. This furnishes the `start' of the inductive
% construction of
% the homotopy in the proof of Lemma 5.11. It is an essentially inescapable
% feature
% of the theory.
% Corollary 5.13. Let P \bullet  (resp., Q\bullet  ) be a bounded-above exact
% complex of projec-
% tives (resp., a bounded-below exact complex of injectives). Then P \bullet
% (resp., Q\bullet  ) is
% homotopy equivalent to the zero-complex.
% Proof. Exercise 5.12.
%  ?
% Corollary 5.13 is the first manifestation of the principle captured more fully
% by Theorem 5.9: we have just verified that, for suitably bounded complexes of
% projectives or injectives, `quasi-isomorphic to 0' is the same as `homotopy
% equivalent
% to 0'.
% Another consequence of Lemma 5.11 is the following remark, showing that
% quasi-isomorphisms are `non-zero-divisors' up to homotopy, with respect to mor-
% phisms from complexes of projectives. This will be useful in the next section,
% for
% example to show that certain lifts are uniquely defined up to homotopy.
% Lemma 5.14. Let A be an abelian category, and let \rho \bullet  : L\bullet  \to
% M \bullet  be a quasi-
% \bullet isomorphism in C(A). Let P be a bounded-above complex of projectives, and let

% P \bullet 
%  \alpha \bullet 
%  / L\bullet 
%  \rho \bullet 
%  / M \bullet 
% q-iso.
% is homotopic to the zero-morphism. Then \alpha \bullet  \sim  0.
% Proof. Let hi : P i \to  M i-1 define a homotopy between \rho \bullet  \circ

%  (\S{}4.1),

% \beta i = (\alpha i , hi ) : P i \to  Li \oplus  M i-1 = M C(\rho )i-1 :
% \cdot \cdot \cdot 
%  / P i-1
%  di-1
%  P \bullet 
%  / P i
%  diP \bullet 
%  / P i+1

%  \beta  i+1

%  -di-1 M C(\rho )\bullet 
%  \sqcup

% I claim that these give a morphism of cochain complexes P \bullet  \to  M C(\rho
% )[-1]\bullet  . In-
% deed24,
% \beta i+1 \circ  diP \bullet  = (\alpha  i+1 \circ  diP \bullet  , hi+1 \circ  diP \bullet  ) and

% negative sign will now come in very handy.

% \bullet

% \alpha \bullet  \sim  0, concluding the
% proof.

% Example 5.15. To see that \alpha \bullet  may not be zero on the nose even if

\subsection{Proof of Theorem 5.9}
\label{subsec:homol:proof-of-theorem-5-9}
% Theorem 5.9 will follow from a more general obser-

% vation: a quasi-isomorphism to a complex of projectives, resp., from a complex
% of
% injectives25, has a right, resp., left, homotopy inverse.
% Proposition 5.16. Let A be an abelian category, and let L\bullet  be a complex
% in C(A).
% Let P \bullet  in C-(P) be a bounded-above complex of projectives, and let
% \alpha \bullet  : L\bullet  \to  P \bullet
% be a quasi-isomorphism. Then there exists a morphism of complexes \beta  \bullet
% : P \bullet  \to  L\bullet
% such that \alpha \bullet  \circ  \beta  \bullet  is homotopic to idP \bullet  .
% (For the injective version, if Q\bullet  is a bounded-below complex of
% injectives and
% \alpha  \bullet 
%  : Q\bullet  \to  L\bullet  is a quasi-isomorphism, then there exists a morphism
%  \beta  \bullet  : L\bullet  \to  Q\bullet
% such that \beta  \bullet  \circ  \alpha \bullet  \sim  idQ\bullet  .)
% Proof. Since \alpha \bullet  : L\bullet  \to  P \bullet  is a quasi-isomorphism,
% the mapping cone M C(\alpha )\bullet
% of \alpha  is an exact complex (Corollary 4.2). Let \rho \bullet  be the
% morphism of complexes
% \rho \bullet  = (0, idP \bullet  ) : P \bullet  \to  L[1]\bullet  \oplus  P \bullet  = M C(\alpha )\bullet  .
% Since M C(\alpha )\bullet  is exact, \rho \bullet  is homotopic to zero by
% Corollary 5.12. Therefore, there
% exist morphisms
% \hat{h}i : P i \to  M C(\alpha )i-1 = Li \oplus  P i-1
% such that
% (*)
%  \rho i = dM
%  i-1
%  C(\alpha )\bullet  \circ  \hat{h}i
%  + \hat{h}
% i+1
%  \circ  dP i
%  \bullet  :
% 25 That is, going `the wrong way': projectives like being the sources of
% morphisms, and
% injectives like to be targets.
% di-1
%  P \bullet 
%  di
%  P \bullet 
% \cdot \cdot \cdot 
%  / P i-1
%  / P i
%  / P i+1
%  / \cdot \cdot \cdot 
% {xx
%  x
%  x
%  xx xx x
%  \rho 
%  x
%  i-1
%  x
%  x
%  \sqcup  i-1
%  yrr
%  r
%  rr
%  \hat{h}ir
%  rr
%  r
%  r
%  r
%  r
%  \rho i
%  \sqcup 
%  i
%  q
%  \hat{h}i+1 q
%  qq
%  qqq
%  q
%  q
%  \rho i+1
%  q
%  q
%  \sqcup  i+1
%  v
%  vv
%  vv
%  v
%  v
%  vv
% xqq
%  zvv
% \cdot \cdot \cdot 
%  / Li \oplus  P / Li+1 \oplus  P / Li+2 \oplus  P / \cdot \cdot \cdot 
% i-1
%  i
% dM C(\alpha )\bullet 
%  dMC(\alpha )\bullet 
% Now we unravel what this says. Write \hat{h}i out in components:
% \hat{h}i = (\beta i , hi )
% with \beta  i : P i \to  Li and hi : P i \to  P i-1 . I claim that
% (i) the collection {\beta  i } is a morphism of cochain complexes: \beta  i+1
% \circ  diP \bullet  = diL\bullet  \circ  \beta  i
% for all i and
% (ii) the morphisms hi give a homotopy \alpha \bullet  \circ  \beta \bullet  \sim
% idP \bullet  .
% Indeed, the left-hand side of (*) is
% \rho i = (0, idP i );
% the right-hand side is
% dM
%  i-1
%  C(\alpha )\bullet  \circ (\beta  i
% , hi
%  ) + (\beta 
%  i+1
%  , hi+1) \circ  diP \bullet 
% = (-diL\bullet  \circ  \beta  i , \alpha i \circ  \beta  i + d i-1
%  P \bullet  \circ  hi
%  ) + (\beta 
%  i+1
%  \circ  dP i
%  \bullet  , h
% i+1
%  \circ  dP i
%  \bullet  ).
% It follows that (*) amounts to
% ?\beta 
%  i+1 \circ  dP i
%  \bullet  - dL\bullet  i
%  \circ  \beta  i
%  = 0,
% idP i - \alpha i \circ  \beta  i = di-1
%  P \bullet  \circ  hi
%  + h
%  i+1
%  \circ  d i
%  P \bullet  ,
% that is, precisely (i) and (ii).
% The morphism of complexes \beta \bullet  : P \bullet  \to  L\bullet  is the
% needed homotopy right-inverse
% of \alpha \bullet 
% .
%  ?
% Taking L\bullet  to be a suitably bounded complex of projectives or injectives
% finally
% establishes Theorem 5.9:
% Proof of Theorem 5.9. We will give the argument for projectives.
% By Proposition 5.16, since P1 \bullet  is in C- (P) and \alpha \bullet  : P0
% \bullet  \to  P1 \bullet  is a quasi-
% isomorphism, there exists a morphism of complexes \beta  \bullet  : P1 \bullet
% \to  P0 \bullet  such that
% \alpha \bullet  \circ  \beta  \bullet  \sim  idP1 \bullet  .
% This implies that H \bullet  (\alpha \bullet  ) \circ  H \bullet  (\beta \bullet
% ) = id (Proposition 4.10). Since H \bullet  (\alpha \bullet  ) is invert-
% \bullet  \bullet  \bullet  \bullet  -ible, so is H (\beta  ): therefore, \beta  is a quasi-isomorphism. Now P0 is also in C (P);
% hence by Proposition 5.16 there exists a morphism of complexes \alpha  \bullet
% \setminus  : P0 \bullet  \to  P1 \bullet
% such that \beta  \bullet  \circ  \alpha \bullet  \setminus  \sim  idP0 \bullet
% . Since
% \alpha \bullet  \setminus  \sim  (\alpha \bullet  \circ  \beta  \bullet  ) \circ  \alpha \bullet  \setminus  = \alpha  \bullet  \circ (\beta \bullet  \circ  \alpha \bullet \setminus  ) \sim  \alpha \bullet  ,
% (Exercise 4.6), it follows that \beta \bullet  \circ  \alpha \bullet  \sim  idP0
% \bullet  . Thus \alpha \bullet  is a homotopy equivalence,
% as stated.
%  ?

\subsection*{Exercises}

% 5.1. ? Prove that the homotopic categories K(A), K-(A), K+ (A) of an abelian
% category A are additive. [\S{}5.2]
% 5.2. ? Prove Proposition 5.4. [\S{}5.2]
% 5.3. Provide a reasonable definition of `projective' and `injective' objects in
% any
% category, and prove that, according to your definition, every set is both
% projective
% and injective in Set.
% 5.4. ? Upgrade Exercises VIII.6.4 and VIII.6.15 to objects of any abelian
% category.
% [5.9, 6.3, \S{}7.4]
% 5.5. ? Let F be a nontrivial finite abelian group. Prove that there are exact
% sequences of finite abelian groups
% 0 \to  A1 \to  A2 \to  F \to  0,
%  0 \to  F \to  B1 \to  B2 \to  0
% which do not split.
% Deduce that the category of finite abelian groups has no nontrivial projective
% or injective objects. [\S{}5.3]
% 5.6. ? Let A, B be abelian categories, and let F : A \to  B, G : B \to  A be
% additive
% functors. Assume that F is left-adjoint to G and that G preserves epimorphisms.
% Prove that if P is a projective object in A, then F (P ) is a projective object
% in B.
% Formulate an analogous result for injective objects. [5.8, \S{}7.1]
% 5.7. \lnot  Let A be an abelian category, and let C be a small category. Assume
% that
% A contains all products indexed by any set26 ; for example, R-Mod satisfies this
% condition for every ring R. For an object A of A and a set S, denote by AS the
% product of A indexed by S. Note that any set-map S \to  T determines a morphism
% AT \to  AS in A.
% For any object X of C, we will define a functor X
%  / : A \to  AC; a much more
% simple-minded functor X : AC
%  \to  A was defined in Exercise 1.11.
% For every object A of A, X
%  / (A) must be an object of AC , that is, a functor
% C \to  A. The value of this functor at the object Y of C is prescribed to be
% X
%  / (A)Y := AHomC (Y,X).
% Every morphism Y \to  Z in C determines a function HomC (Z, X) \to  HomC (Y, X),
% and this defines a morphism X
%  / (A)Y \to  X
%  / (A)Z.
% \bullet  Prove that this prescription defines X
%  / (A) as a functor C \to  A.
% \bullet  Prove that X
%  / is a functor A \to  AC
%  , with the evident action on morphisms.
% \bullet  Prove that X
%  / is right-adjoint to X .
% 26Recall that `infinite' products may be defined as limits; see Example
% VIII.1.10.
% (Hint: You have to identify HomAC (F , X
%  / (A)) with Hom A(F (X), A). Given a
% natural transformation \alpha  : F \to  X /
%  (A), evaluate at X and extract the identity
% component to obtain a morphism F (X) \to  A. Then show that this one piece of
% information determines the whole natural transformation.) [5.8, 5.9]
% 5.8. \lnot  As in Exercise 5.7, let C be a small category and let A be an
% abelian cate-
% gory containing all products indexed by sets. Prove that the functor X
%  / preserves
% injectives for all objects X of C: if Q is an injective object of A, then X
%  / (Q) is
% C
% injective in A . (Hint: Exercise 5.6.) [5.9]
% 5.9. \lnot  Let C be a small category, and let A be an abelian category
% containing all
% products indexed by sets. Assume that A has enough injectives. Let F : C \to  A
% be
% a functor. For every object X of C, let QX be an injective object of A admitting
% a
% monomorphism F (X) / / QX .
% ?
% \bullet  With notation as in Exercise 5.7, let Q := X\in Obj(C) X
%  / (QX ) (prove that this
% product exists in AC ).
% \bullet  Prove that Q is injective in AC . (Exercises 5.4 and 5.8.)
% \bullet  Define a morphism F \to  Q, and prove it is a monomorphism. (Use adjunction.)
% Therefore, we can conclude that AC has enough injectives if A is an abelian
% category
% with enough injectives and A is closed with respect to products indexed by sets.
% Prove that if A is an abelian category with enough projectives and A is closed
% with respect to coproducts indexed by sets, then AC has enough projectives.
% (Hint:
% (AC )op \sim 
%  = (Aop )(Cop
% ).) [5.10]
% 5.10. Prove that the category of presheaves of abelian groups on a topological
% space has enough injectives and enough projectives27 . (Use Exercise 5.9.)
% \bullet 5.11. ? Let N be the complex
% \cdot \cdot \cdot 
%  / 0
%  / K
%  / M
%  / N
%  / 0
%  / \cdot \cdot \cdot  .
% \bullet Prove that N is exact and splits (in a sense analogous to the one given for modules
% in \S{}III.7.2) if and only if idN \bullet  is homotopic to 0 (cf. Proposition
% III.7.5). Deduce
% that every additive functor sends split exact sequences to split exact
% sequences.
% \bullet More generally, let N be any complex. Prove that idN \bullet  is homotopic to 0
% (that is, N \bullet  is `split exact' as defined in \S{}5.4) if and only if N
% \bullet  is isomorphic to a
% complex of the form
% \cdot \cdot \cdot 
%  d-3 /
%  M -2 \oplus  M -1
%  d-2 /
%  M -1 \oplus  M 0
%  d-1 /
%  M 0 \oplus  M 1
%  d0
%  / M 1 \oplus  M 2
%  d1
%  / \cdot \cdot \cdot 
% where di is 0 on the M i factor, and (idM i+1 , 0) on the M i+1 factor.
% [\S{}5.4, 6.3, \S{}7.4]
% 5.12. ? Prove that every exact, bounded-above complex of projectives is homotopy
% equivalent to zero. Prove that every exact, bounded-below complex of injectives
% is
% homotopy equivalent to zero. [\S{}5.4]
% 27 The category of sheaves of abelian groups on a topological space also has
% enough injectives,
% but in general it does not have enough projectives.
% 5.13. Let A be an abelian category, and let \alpha \bullet  : L\bullet  \to  M
% \bullet  be a quasi-isomorphism
% in C(A). Assume that \beta  \bullet  is a homotopy one-sided inverse of \alpha
% \bullet . Prove that \beta \bullet  is
% also a quasi-isomorphism.
% 5.14. \lnot  Let P0 \bullet , P1 \bullet  be bounded-above complexes of
% projective objects in an abelian
% category A. (You may assume A has enough projectives, if that helps.) Let
% L\bullet  be
% a (not necessarily bounded) complex in C(A).
% \bullet  Assume that there are quasi-isomorphisms
% q-iso.
%  L\bullet >
%  > > > q-iso.
%  >
%  ?
% \bullet 
%  \bullet 
% P0 P1\bullet  \bullet Prove that P0 and P1 are homotopy equivalent.
% \bullet  Assume that there are quasi-isomorphisms
% \bullet 
%  \bullet 
% q-iso. P0 > >
%  > >
%  ?
%  q-iso.
%  P1L\bullet 
% \bullet  \bullet Prove that P0 and P1 are homotopy equivalent. (This may turn out to be
% challenging. If so, wait until you have covered \S{}6, in particular Theorem
% 6.6.)
% [6.5]

\section{Projective and injective resolutions and the derived category}
\label{sec:homol:projective-and-injective-resolutions-and-the-derived-category}

% Theorem 5.9 is very good news. It tells us that if we are willing and able to
% change
% our viewpoint and adopt bounded complexes of projectives or injectives as our
% natural environment, then we will have an excellent handle on the subtle notion
% of quasi-isomorphism: because quasi-isomorphisms become homotopy equivalences
% in that environment and homotopy equivalences are better behaved than arbitrary
% quasi-isomorphisms. For example, homotopy equivalence of complexes is manifestly
% an equivalence relation (Exercise 4.4), while quasi-isomorphism is not (see the
% dis-
% cussion following Example 4.6). Further, homotopy equivalences are preserved by
% every additive functor (Theorem 4.14), while general quasi-isomorphisms are not
% (Exercise 4.15).
% The question is therefore how to switch to a homotopic environment and adopt
% complexes of projectives or injectives as our primary object of study, while
% keeping
% intact the essential information carried by the objects of A or even the
% complexes
% in C(A).
% This can be done if A has enough projectives or enough injectives; in this
% situation, we will be able to produce an adequate description of the derived
% category
% (\S{}6.3).

\subsection{Recovering A}
\label{subsec:homol:recovering-a}
% Recall once more that a `copy' of an abelian category A is

% available within C(A), by associating with every object A in A the complex \iota
% (A)
% having A in degree 0 and 0 elsewhere. Composing with the functor C(A) \to  K(A)
% realizes A as a full subcategory of K(A) (Exercise 6.1). We will look for `more
% interesting' copies of A in the homotopic category.
% Let us begin by recalling (and sharpening) our definition of
% projective/injective
% resolutions of an object A.
% Definition 6.1. Let A be an object of an abelian category A.
% A projective resolution of A is a quasi-isomorphism P \bullet  \to  \iota (A),
% where P \bullet  is a
% \leq 0complex in C (P).
% An injective resolution of A is a quasi-isomorphism \iota (A) \to  Q\bullet  ,
% where Q\bullet  is a
% complex in C\geq 0 (I).
%  ?
% By common abuse of language, I will often refer to P \bullet  or Q\bullet  as
% the resolution,
% leaving the quasi-isomorphism understood.
% Remark 6.2. The terminology is potentially confusing, since it hints that the
% resolutions themselves may be projective/injective as objects of the abelian
% cate-
% gory C(A), or of its bounded variations. This is not the case (Exercise 6.3).
%  ?
% Projective or injective resolutions need not exist: as I pointed out (Exam-
% ple 5.6), an abelian category may have no nontrivial projective or injective
% objects.
% It is however clear that such resolutions exist if the category has enough
% projec-
% tives/injectives: if A has enough projectives, then given any object A of A
% there
% is a projective P 0 with an epimorphism \pi  : P 0 \to  A; and then a projective
% P -1
% with an epimorphism d-1 : P -1 \to  ker \pi ; and then a projective P -2 with an
% epimorphism d-2 : P -2 \to  ker d-1 ; and so on. Letting diP \bullet  be the
% composition
% P i / ker di+1 / / P i+1 gives
% d-2
%  P \bullet 
%  d-1
%  P \bullet 
% \cdot \cdot \cdot 
%  / P -2
%  / P -1
%  / P 0
%  / 0
%  / \cdot \cdot \cdot  ,
% a projective resolution of A. Just as clearly, if A has enough injectives, then
% every
% object A has an injective resolution.
% Now consider the category of homotopy classes of quasi-isomorphisms with tar-
% get \iota (A) (cf. Remark 4.5): this is a `homotopic category of resolutions' of
% A. I claim
% that projective resolutions, if they exist, are initial in this category.
% (Analogously,
% injective resolutions are final in the homotopic category of quasi-isomorphism
% with
% source \iota (A).)
% As the reader knows, this means that projective resolutions are supposed to
% map uniquely (in K(A)) to every resolution of A. The following key lemma proves
% this and more:
% \bullet Lemma 6.3. Let A be an object of an abelian category A. Let M be a resolution
% \bullet  \leq 0of A, and let P be any complex in C (P). Let
% \phi  : H 0 (P \bullet  ) \to  H 0 (M \bullet  ) = A
% be an arbitrary morphism. Then
% \bullet  there exist morphisms of complexes \alpha \bullet  : P \bullet  \to  M \bullet  inducing \phi  at the level
% of H 0 ;
% \bullet  different morphisms satisfying the previous requirement are necessarily homo-
% topy equivalent.
% Of course an analogous statement holds for complexes Q\bullet  in C\geq 0 (I),
% giving
% morphisms of cochain complexes M \bullet  \to  Q\bullet  inducing a given
% morphism in H 0.
% Proof. We have to define \alpha i : P i \to  M i for all i. Since \alpha i = 0
% necessarily for i > 0,
% \bullet we may as well replace M with its truncated version (cf. Exercise 3.1) and then
% \bullet extend both P and this complex as follows:
% -2
%  -1
% \cdot \cdot \cdot 
%  / P -2
%  dP \bullet 
%  / P -1
%  dP \bullet 
%  / P 0
%  \pi 
%  / H 0(P \bullet  )
%  / 0
% \alpha -2
%  \alpha -1
%  \alpha 0
%  \phi 
% \sqcup 
%  d-2
%  M \bullet 
%  \sqcup 
%  d-1
%  M \bullet 
%  \sqcup 
%  \mu 
%  \sqcup 
% \cdot \cdot \cdot 
%  / M -2
%  / M -1
%  / ker d0 M
%  \bullet 
%  / H 0 (M \bullet  ) = A
%  / 0
% Note that the bottom complex is then exact. The morphism \phi  is given to us,
% and
% the task is to define the dotted lifts \alpha i , i \leq  0, so as to obtain a
% morphism of
% complexes.
% Now, P 0 is projective and maps to A by \phi  \circ  \pi ; this guarantees the
% existence of
% a lifting \alpha 0 of \phi . (The map P 0 \to  M 0 to the degree-0 term of the
% original complex
% is obtained by following with ker d0 M \bullet  / / M 0 .) Next, note that
% \mu  \circ  \alpha 0 \circ  d-1
%  P \bullet  = \phi  \circ  \pi  \circ  dP -1
%  \bullet  = 0;
% therefore, \alpha 0 \circ  d P -1
%  \bullet  factors through ker \mu . Since the bottom complex is exact, ker \mu  =
% -1
% im dM
%  \bullet 
%  .
%  Therefore,
%  we have the diagram
% -1
% P0 -1
% \alpha  \circ  dP \bullet 
% d-1
%  M \bullet 
%  \sqcup 
% M -1
%  / im d-1 M
%  \bullet 
%  / 0
% and a lift \alpha -1 then exists since P -1 is projective.
% The construction of the other \alpha -i, i > 1, proceeds inductively in
% precisely the
% same way, using at each step the fact that the bottom complex is exact.
% This proves the existence of \alpha \bullet  : P \bullet  \to  M \bullet  . Its
% uniqueness up to homotopy
% follows immediately from Lemma 5.11.
%  ?
% Summarizing, if A contains enough projectives, then every object A of A ad-
% mits a projective resolution, and further this is initial among all resolutions
% with
% target \iota (A). If A has enough injectives, then every object A of A admits an
% injective
% resolution, and this is final among all resolutions with source \iota (A).
% Recall that initial, resp., final, objects of a category are always unique up to
% isomorphism (Proposition I.5.4). Here, this says the following:
% Proposition 6.4. Any two projective (resp., injective) resolutions of an object
% A
% of an abelian category A are homotopy equivalent.
% (This is also a direct consequence of Lemma 6.3.)
% The moral I extract from these considerations is that if an abelian category A
% has enough (say) projectives, then we can associate with each object A of A an
% \leq 0object of K (P), determined up to homotopy. This can in fact be done functorially,
% in the sense that morphisms in A can be lifted to morphisms of corresponding
% projective resolutions:
% \bullet Proposition 6.5. Let A0 , A1 be objects of an abelian category A, and let Pi be
% a projective resolution of Ai, i = 0, 1. Then every morphism \phi  : A0 \to  A1
% in A is
% induced by a morphism \alpha \bullet  : P0 \bullet  \to  P1 \bullet  , uniquely
% determined up to homotopy.
% Of course an analogous statement holds for injective resolutions.
% Proof. By hypothesis, \phi  is a morphism H 0 (P0 \bullet  ) \to  H 0 (P1
% \bullet  ). The complex P0 \bullet
% consists of projectives, and P1 \bullet  is a resolution of A1 ; therefore a
% lift \alpha \bullet  exists and
% is unique up to homotopy, as an immediate consequence of Lemma 6.3.
%  ?
% The assignment provided by Proposition 6.5 is clearly covariant. The bottom
% line is that the part of A consisting of objects with (say) projective
% resolutions
% -is equivalent to a full subcategory of K (P). Dear reader, please formalize
% this
% assertion, for it is a key point (Exercise 6.2). If A has enough projectives,
% this gives
% -the promised `copy' of A within K (P).
% Of course the situation is entirely analogous concerning the subcategory of A
% consisting of objects admitting an injective resolution and K+ (I).

\subsection{From objects to complexes}
\label{subsec:homol:from-objects-to-complexes}
% Suppose that an abelian category A has

% enough projectives. At this point we hopefully agree (Exercise 6.2) that the ho-
% -motopic category K (P) contains a full subcategory which is equivalent to A:
% the
% (homotopy) category of projective resolutions of objects of A. While a priori
% each
% object of A corresponds to many projective resolutions, these are all homotopy
% -equivalent to one another (by Proposition 6.4), hence isomorphic in K (P); mor-
% phisms in A correspond precisely to homotopy classes of morphisms between pro-
% -jective resolutions (by Proposition 6.5), hence to morphisms in K (P).
% -In practice, this says that we can use this subcategory of K (P) as a replace-
% ment for the original abelian category A. We are very close to achieving our
% goal of
% identifying the `essential nature' of cohomology: since homotopy equivalent com-
% plexes have the same cohomology (by Theorem 4.14) and additive functors preserve
% -homotopy equivalence, objects of K (P) are ideal carriers of cohomology
% invariants:
% while applying an additive functor to objects of A in general destroys
% cohomolog-
% -ical information, applying `the same functor' to a corresponding object in K
% (P)
% preserves that information.
% We will follow this lead in the next section; the functors Tori and Exti encoun-
% tered in Chapter VIII will arise precisely in this fashion and will stand as our
% poster
% examples of derived functors.
% Of course, exactly the same situation will occur if A has enough injectives, in
% which case the appropriate replacement for A is found in K+ (I). I will say a
% word
% about the general situation when projectives or injectives are not available, in
% \S{}9.1.
% The reader should now wonder whether the categories K- (P), K+ (I) may in fact
% be used to extract cohomological information for any (bounded) complex, not just
% for resolutions of a given object of A. As observed in \S{}4.2, resolutions (in
% C- (A)
% or C+ (A)) are just special cases of quasi-isomorphisms; the natural question is
% -whether every (suitably bounded) complex has a `quasi-isomorphic copy' in K (P)
% or K+ (I), again uniquely defined up to isomorphism in the homotopy category
% (that
% is, up to homotopy equivalence in C(A)).
% This is indeed the case.
% Theorem 6.6. Assume the abelian category A has enough projectives, and let
% L\bullet  be
% - \bullet 
% a complex in C (A). Then there exists a bounded-above complex of projectives
% Pand a quasi-isomorphism P \bullet  \to  L\bullet  , and P \bullet  is uniquely
% defined up to homotopy
% equivalence. Further, every morphism \alpha  \bullet  of complexes in C-(A)
% lifts to a morphism
% of the corresponding projective resolutions in K- (P), also uniquely determined
% (up
% to homotopy).
% The lift obtained in the last part of this statement exists in the homotopy
% category: any representative in C-(P) will be a `homotopy lift' of \alpha
% \bullet  , in the sense
% that the relevant diagram will only be guaranteed to commute up to homotopy.
% I will call a complex P \bullet  as in the statement a `projective resolution of
% L\bullet  ';
% this is a slight abuse of language, since it forgets the specific
% quasi-isomorphism
% P \bullet  \to  L\bullet  .
% As always, there is a mirror statement to Theorem 6.6 for injectives, which I
% will leave to the reader to formulate precisely. It is a particularly good
% exercise for
% the reader to construct a stand-alone proof for the injective case.
% Proof. The proof of this result is admittedly rather technical, as it involves
% many
% of the tools that we have developed.
% Construction of P \bullet  . We may assume that L\bullet  is in C\leq 0 (A),
% -2
%  -1
% \cdot \cdot \cdot 
%  / L-2
%  dL\bullet 
%  / L-1
%  dL\bullet 
%  / L0
%  / 0
%  / \cdot \cdot \cdot  ,
% and we are seeking a complex P \bullet  in C\leq 0 (P) and a quasi-isomorphism
% \lambda \bullet  : P \bullet  \to  L\bullet  :
% -2
%  -1
% \cdot \cdot \cdot 
%  / P -2
%  dP \bullet 
%  / P -1
%  dP \bullet 
%  / P 0
%  / 0
%  / \cdot \cdot \cdot 
% \lambda -2
%  \lambda -1
%  \lambda 0
%  \lambda 1
% \sqcup 
%  d-2
%  L\bullet 
%  \sqcup 
%  d-1
%  L\bullet 
%  \sqcup 
%  \sqcup 
% \cdot \cdot \cdot 
%  / L-2
%  / L
% -1
%  / L0
%  / 0
%  / \cdot \cdot \cdot 
% \bullet The construction will give us a little more: we will obtain a projective P and
% an epi morphism \lambda \bullet  : P \bullet  \to  L\bullet  and moreover such
% that each induced morphism
% \hat{\lambda }i : ker diP \bullet  \to  ker diL\bullet  is also an epimorphism.
% For i > 0, necessarily \lambda i = 0. Arguing inductively, we may assume we have
% already constructed a suitable \lambda i+1 and use it to construct \lambda i .
% Here is the diagram
% summarizing this inductive step:
% diP \bullet 
% P i G
%  G G
%  G G
%  G \lambda 
%  /G
%  / i
%  G Li G
%  G G
%  \times 
%  G G
%  ker G G G di+1
%  \# \#
%  L\bullet 
%  \sqcup 
%  ker di+1
%  P \bullet 
%  \pi 
%  / ker di+1
%  \sqcup 
%  P
%  \hat{\lambda }i+1
%  \bullet 
%  /
%  +/
%  P i+1
% Li
%  i
%  / ker di+1
%  L
% \bullet 
% dL\bullet 
% Here, diL\bullet  restricts the target of diL\bullet  . The square is a
% pull-back (cf. Examples 1.11
% and 2.2). The morphism \hat{\lambda }i+1 on the right is an epimorphism by
% induction, and it
% follows that the morphism on the left is also an epimorphism, by Lemma 2.3. A
% projective object P i with an epimorphism to the fibered product exists because
% A
% has enough projectives; the composition \lambda i : P i \to  Li is then also an
% epimorphism,
% as claimed. Since the square is a pull-back, the kernel of \pi  maps
% isomorphically
% i
% to the kernel of dL
% \bullet  (Exercise 1.16); it follows easily that the new induced map
% \hat{\lambda }i
%  : ker dP i
%  \bullet  \to  ker diL\bullet  is an epimorphism, concluding the inductive step and the
% construction of the complex P \bullet  and of the morphism \lambda \bullet  .
% To see that the morphism induced in cohomology by \lambda \bullet  is an
% isomorphism,
% look again at the pull-back diagram and note that H i+1 (P \bullet  ), resp., H
% i+1 (L\bullet  ), may
% be realized as the cokernel of \pi , resp., dL
% \bullet  i
%  . Since \hat{\lambda }
% i+1
%  is an epimorphism, so is the
% morphism
% (diL\bullet  , -\hat{\lambda }i+1 ) : Li \oplus  ker di+1
%  P \bullet  -\to  ker dL\bullet  i+1
%  .
% As the diagram is a pull-back, this implies (as observed in Example 2.2) that it
% is
% a push-out as well, and (Exercise 1.16 again) it follows that the induced
% morphism
% coker \pi  \to  coker diL\bullet  is an isomorphism. As noted, this is nothing
% but
% H i+1 (\lambda \bullet  )
%  :
%  H i+1 (P \bullet  ) -\to  H i+1 (L\bullet  ),
% so we are done.
% \bullet 
%  \leq 0Uniqueness up to homotopy. It suffices to compare an arbitrary P in C (P)
% mapping to L\bullet  with the complex P \bullet  constructed in the first part
% of the proof.
% Working on the same diagram used above,
% P \exists \tilde{\lambda } S' i i ''
%  N ''
% '
%  N
%  '
% ''
% N N i ? N
%  ?
%  N
%  v
%  ?
%  /N v / N v Li v
%  N v N \setminus  v
%  N \times 
%  v
%  N
%  |
%  v
%  ker N v
%  <
% N v
%  N v
%  d & & : i+1
%  L\bullet 
%  L

%  i
%  ker di+1

%  // ker ker > di+1
%  di+1

%  P
%  \bullet

%  i+1
%  ? ?
%  ?
%  ?
%  / ? / P Li+1
%  ?
%  ? ?
%  C
%  i+1
%  \sqcup 
% ''''
% ''P
%  / PI claim that the morphism \lambda \bullet 
%  :P \bullet 
%  \to  L\bullet  lifts to a morphism \tilde{\lambda }\bullet  :P \bullet 
%  \to  P \bullet  . Indeed,
% both complexes are 0 in degree ? 0; thus, for i ? 0, P i
%  \to  P i is necessarily
% the zero-morphism. Arguing inductively once more, we assume the lift has been
% constructed in all degrees > i, and we construct it in degree i.
% The composition P i
%  \to  P
%  i+1
%  \to  P i+1 \to  P i+2 agrees with the composition
% P i
%  \to  P
%  i+1
%  \to  P
%  i+2
%  \to  P i+2
%  , so it is the zero-morphism. Therefore this composition
% factors through ker di+1
%  P \bullet  : this gives the dotted morphism in the diagram. By the
% universal property of fibered products, we obtain the dashed morphism. Since P i
% maps epimorphically to Li \times ker di+1
%  L
% \bullet 
%  ker di+1
%  P \bullet  , the needed lift \tilde{\lambda }i
%  : P i
%  \to  P i
%  exists as
% i
% P is projective. The `outer' diagram is commutative by construction; hence the
% collection \tilde{\lambda }i gives a morphism of cochain complexes
% \tilde{\lambda }\bullet  . Note that so far we have
% \bullet 
% not used the hypothesis that \lambda  is a quasi-isomorphism.
% -We now have the following commutative diagram in C (A):
% \bullet 
% \bullet 
%  |
%  \tilde{\lambda } | \bullet 
%  |
%  |
%  | |
%  |
%  | P =
%  \sqcup \sqcup 
%  \lambda \bullet 
% P
%  / L\bullet 
% \bullet 
% \lambda 
% Both \lambda \bullet  and \lambda \bullet 
%  are quasi-isomorphisms; therefore so is \tilde{\lambda }\bullet  . Theorem 5.9
%  implies
% then that \tilde{\lambda }\bullet  is a homotopy equivalence, as needed.
% Lifting morphisms. Let \alpha \bullet  : L\bullet 
%  \to  L\bullet  be any morphism in C- (A). The argu-
% ment we have just given shows that if P \bullet 
%  is in C- (P) and L \bullet  is in C- (A), then every
% morphism P \bullet 
%  \to  L\bullet  lifts to a morphism P \bullet 
%  \to  P \bullet  , where P \bullet  is the complex con-
% structed in the first part of the proof. In fact, we have now established that
% there
% exists a homotopy lift28 to any complex P \bullet  mapping quasi-isomorphically
% to L\bullet  ,
% since every such complex is homotopy equivalent to the one constructed earlier.
% \bullet 
% Applying this observation to any complex P
%  mapping quasi-isomorphically
% \bullet 
%  \bullet to L lifts \alpha  as needed.
% Finally, assume that both \alpha 0 \bullet  , \alpha 1 \bullet  lift \alpha
% \bullet  homotopically:
% \alpha \bullet 
% P
%  \bullet 
%  // P \bullet 
% \alpha \bullet 
% q-iso.
%  \lambda \bullet  q-iso.
% \sqcup 
%  \bullet 
%  \alpha 
% \bullet 
%  \sqcup 
% L
%  / L\bullet 
% This means that \lambda \bullet  \circ (\alpha 1 \bullet  - \alpha 0 \bullet  )
% is homotopic to 0:
% P
%  \bullet 
%  \alpha \bullet 
%  1 -\alpha 0
%  \bullet 
%  / P \bullet 
%  q-iso.
%  5/ L\bullet 
% \sim 0
% It follows that \alpha 1 \bullet  - \alpha 0 \bullet  \sim  0, by Lemma 5.14,
% and this concludes the proof.
%  ?
% 28 That is, the corresponding diagram will commute up to homotopy.
%  In general, a lift
% in C- (P) may not exist; cf. Exercise 6.10.

\subsection{Poor man's derived category}
\label{subsec:homol:poor-man-s-derived-category}
% We are now in a position to close a relatively

% large circle of ideas. I claim that if the abelian category A has enough
% projectives,
% resp., injectives, then K- (P), resp., K+ (I), is a solution `up to
% isomorphisms' of the
% universal problem presented in \S{}4.2. The precise statement is the following.
% By Proposition 5.4, the functor C(A) \to  D(A) factors through the homotopic
% category; this of course holds for the bounded versions of these categories as
% well.
% Thus, we have functors K- (A) \to  D-(A) and K+ (A) \to  D+ (A). By restriction,
% we
% obtain functors
% K-(P) \to  D-(A),
%  K+ (I) \to  D+ (A).
% Theorem 6.7. Let A be an abelian category with enough projectives. Then the
% functor K- (P) \to  D- (A) is an equivalence of categories.
% If A has enough injectives, then the functor K+ (I) \to  D+ (A) is an
% equivalence
% of categories.
% Theorem 6.7 is proven in full detail in any more complete treatment of homo-
% logical algebra. Since we have not actually constructed D- (A), we cannot really
% prove this statement here; but we now know enough to appreciate why Theorem 6.7
% should be true, in the sense that K- (P), K+(I) satisfy the expected universal
% prop-
% erties. I will deal with the projective side of the story, since the injective
% side
% mirrors it faithfully.
% Assume that A has enough projectives. Define a functor P : C- (A) \to 
% K-
%  (P) by associating with every bounded-above complex L\bullet  any projective
%  res-
% olution P(L\bullet  ) of L\bullet  (which exists by Theorem 6.6) and with every
% morphism
% \alpha  \bullet  : L\bullet  \to  M \bullet  in C- (A) the morphism P(\alpha \bullet  ) in K- (P) lifting \alpha \bullet  .
% The morphism P(\alpha \bullet  ) is the homotopy class of a homotopy lift of
% \alpha \bullet  ; we have
% proved that any two such lifts are homotopy equivalent. It also follows that P
% is
% indeed a functor: for example, if \alpha \bullet  : L\bullet  \to  M \bullet
% and \beta  \bullet  : M \bullet  \to  N \bullet  are morphisms
% in C- (A), then both P(\beta \bullet  ) \circ  P(\alpha  \bullet  ) and P(\beta
% \bullet  \circ  \alpha \bullet  ) are classes of homotopy lifts
% of \beta  \bullet  \circ  \alpha \bullet  ; therefore P(\beta  \bullet  ) \circ
% P(\alpha \bullet  ) = P(\beta  \bullet  \circ  \alpha \bullet  ) by uniqueness,
% as needed.
% Note that there is a very large element of arbitrariness in the choice of P: I
% am not prescribing any particular recipe for choosing a projective resolution
% rather
% than another. But all such resolutions are homotopically equivalent, as proven
% in
% Theorem 6.6; therefore different choices would only move the resolutions in
% their
% -isomorphism class in K (P).
% Remark 6.8. There is an efficient way to clarify (and defuse) this
% arbitrariness.
% Suppose P, P \setminus  are two choices for the resolution functor C- (A) \to
% K- (P). Thus,
% we have chosen two projective resolutions P(L\bullet  ), P \setminus  (L\bullet
% ) for each object L\bullet
% in C-(A). By Theorem 6.6, there is a homotopy equivalence between P(L\bullet  )
% and P \setminus  (L\bullet  ), that is, an isomorphism \rho L\bullet  :
% P(L\bullet  ) \to  P \setminus  (L\bullet  ) in K-(P); this
% isomorphism is unique (since it lifts the identity on L\bullet  and lifts are
% unique up to
% homotopy). Further, for every morphism of cochain complexes \alpha \bullet  :
% L\bullet  0 \to  L\bullet  1 , the
% diagram
% P(L\bullet  0 )
%  P(\alpha \bullet  )
%  / P(L\bullet  1 )
% \rho L\bullet 
%  ?
%  ? \rho L\bullet 
% \sqcup 
%  P ? (\alpha \bullet  )
%  \sqcup 
% P \setminus  (L\bullet  0 )
%  / P \setminus  (L\bullet  1 )
% commutes: this is again by the uniqueness of lifts up to homotopy, since \rho
% L\bullet  1 \circ  P(\alpha \bullet  )
% and P \setminus  (\alpha \bullet ) \circ  \rho L\bullet  0 both correspond to
% lifts of \alpha \bullet  to P(L\bullet  0 ) \to  P \setminus (L\bullet  1 ).
% This says that there is a unique natural isomorphism 29 between any two choices
% P, P \setminus  of functors of projective resolutions. In a categorical context,
% this is essen-
% tially as good as uniqueness. Thus the arbitrariness in the choice of P is less
% dramatic than it may seem at first.
%  ?
% Choose any functor P as above. I claim that P : C- (A) \to  K- (P) is
% essentially
% -a solution to the universal problem defining the derived category D (A). Here
% is a
% more precise statement:
% Theorem 6.9. With notation as above, we have the following.
% \bullet  If \rho \bullet  is a quasi-isomorphism in C- (A), then P(\rho \bullet  ) is an isomorphism in K- (P).
% \bullet  Let F : C- (A) -\to  D be an additive functor such that F (\rho \bullet  ) is an iso-
% morphism for every quasi-isomorphism \rho \bullet  . Then there exists a functor
% 0 F
%  :
% K-(P) \to  D, such that the diagram
% C-(A)
%  F
%  / D
% P
%  \sqcup  { { { {
%  {
%  F {
%  ?
%  {
%  { {
%  =
% -K (P)
% commutes up to natural isomorphism. (That is, there is a natural transfor-
% mation \nu  : 0 F
%  \circ  P ; F such that
% \nu L\bullet  : 0 F
%  \circ  P(L\bullet  ) -\to 
%  \sim 
%  F (L\bullet  )
% is an isomorphism for every complex L\bullet  in C- (A).) The functor F
%  0 is unique
% up to natural isomorphism.
% The fact that the diagram does not commute on the nose should not be too
% disturbing. It corresponds to the fact (stated in Theorem 6.7) that there is
% `only'
% - -an equivalence of categories between K (P) and D (A), not a hard and fast
% `iso-
% morphism'. As I already pointed out in \S{}VIII.1.3, this is what should be
% expected
% in a categorical context.
% 29 `Natural transformations' were rapidly defined in \S{}VIII.1.5, Definition
% VIII.1.15. They are
% the most sensible notion of a morphism between functors. A natural isomorphism
% is a natural
% transformation that is an isomorphism on each object.
% Proof. For the first point, let \rho \bullet  : L\bullet  \to  M \bullet  be any
% morphism in C- (A). By
% Theorem 6.6, \rho \bullet  lifts to a morphism of resolutions: we have a diagram
% P(L\bullet  )
% ?\bullet 
% \rho / P(M \bullet  )
% \mu L\bullet 
% \sqcup 
% L\bullet 
% \bullet 
% \rho 
% \mu M \bullet 
% \sqcup 
% / M \bullet 
% which commutes up to homotopy; here \mu L\bullet  , \mu M \bullet  are
% quasi-isomorphisms, and P(\rho \bullet )
% is the homotopy class of \rho \setminus  \bullet  . If \rho \bullet  is a
% quasi-isomorphism, then so is \rho \setminus  \bullet  (since the
% diagram commutes up to homotopy; hence it commutes after taking cohomology);
% it follows that P(\rho  \bullet  ) is an isomorphism in this case, by Corollary
% 5.10.
% To prove the factorization property, define F
%  0 : K- (P) \to  D by setting
% 0(P F
%  \bullet ) := F (P \bullet )
% for any bounded-above complex P \bullet  of projectives, and define 0 F
%  of a morphism
% in K-
%  (P) to be F (\alpha \bullet 
%  ), for any representative \alpha \bullet 
%  of that morphism.
% The fact that the action of 0 F
%  on morphisms is well-defined is precisely the
% content of Lemma 5.1: homotopic morphisms have the same image in D. It is
% immediate that 0 F
%  is a functor, and we have to check the statement about commu-
% tativity up to isomorphism of the diagram and the uniqueness of 0 F
%  up to natural
% isomorphism.
% Then let L\bullet  be a complex in C-(A). By construction there is a
% quasi-isomorphism
% \mu L\bullet  : P(L\bullet  ) \to  L\bullet  . Since F maps quasi-isomorphisms to isomorphisms, the in-
% duced morphism
% \nu L\bullet  : 0(P(L\bullet  F
%  )) = F (P(L\bullet  )) -\to  F (L\bullet )
% is an isomorphism. We just have to verify that the isomorphisms \nu L\bullet
% define a
% natural transformation: that is, that for all morphisms \alpha \bullet  :
% L\bullet  \to  M \bullet  in C- (A) the
% diagram
% F
%  ? (P(\alpha \bullet  ))
% 0(P(L\bullet  F
%  ))
%  / F
%  0(P(M \bullet  ))
% \nu L\bullet 
%  \nu M \bullet 
% \sqcup 
%  F (\alpha \bullet 
%  )
%  \sqcup 
% F (L\bullet )
%  / F (M \bullet  )
% commutes. This diagram (in D) is obtained by applying F to the diagram (in the
% category C(A))
% P(L\bullet  )
%  \alpha ? \bullet 
%  / P(M \bullet  )
% \mu L\bullet 
%  \mu M \bullet 
% \sqcup 
%  \bullet 
%  \sqcup 
% L\bullet 
%  \alpha 
%  / M \bullet 
% where \alpha  \setminus  \bullet 
%  is a homotopy lift of \alpha \bullet  . This diagram commutes up to homotopy,
%  so
% the previous one commutes on the nose, again by Lemma 5.1.
% To see that 0 F
%  is unique up to isomorphism, let F : K- (P) \to  D be another
% functor satisfying the same property. Thus there is a natural isomorphism \nu  :
% F \circ  P ; F . For P \bullet  a complex in K- (P), consider the composition
% \rho P \bullet  :
% F (P \bullet )
%  \sim 
%  / F (P(P \bullet  ))
%  \sim 
%  / F (P \bullet  ) = F
%  0 (P \bullet  )
% F (\mu P \bullet  )-1
%  \nu P \bullet 
% (keep in mind that since \mu P \bullet  : P(P \bullet  ) \to  P \bullet  is a
% quasi-isomorphism, F (\mu P \bullet  ) is
% invertible in D). The reader will verify (Exercise 6.12) that this defines \rho
% : F \to  0
%  F
% as a natural isomorphism, concluding the proof.
%  ?
% If A has enough injectives, we can define a functor Q : C+ (A) \to  K+ (I) by
% choosing an injective resolution for every bounded-below complex. The situation
% concerning this functor is precisely analogous to the situation just reviewed
% for the
% functor P, modulo reversing arrows as appropriate.
% Summary. If an abelian category A has enough projectives (resp., enough in-
% jectives), then the homotopic category K - (P) (resp., K+ (I)) works as a
% replacement
% of the derived category D-(A) (resp., D+ (A)). As I have argued earlier, viewing
% a complex in these categories is the most natural way to extract the maximum
% amount of `cohomological' information from the complex. The category A itself
% (or
% a category equivalent to A) sits as a full subcategory of these categories.
% To reiterate the key point, there is a clear advantage in stopping short of
% taking
% cohomology. Resolving a complex L\bullet  by a complex P \bullet  of (say)
% projectives, thanks
% to Theorem 6.6, gives us an entity which certainly captures the cohomological
% information of L\bullet  but that can still be manipulated with the entire range
% of tools
% \bullet available in dealing with complexes. For example, we can apply to P any additive
% functor and still obtain a complex whose cohomology only depends on the original
% complex L\bullet  (by Theorem 4.14). The cohomology of L\bullet  is only one
% example of the
% cohomological invariants that may be extracted from L\bullet  ; viewing L\bullet
% in the derived
% category gives direct access to all these invariants.
% We will capitalize on these considerations in the next section.

\subsection*{Exercises}

% 6.1. ? Prove that the full subcategory of K(A) consisting of complexes
